<p><b>Guinea</b> <span class="nowrap"><span class="noexcerpt"><a href="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/1/1f/En-us-Guinea.ogg" title="Listen"><img alt="Listen" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Speakerlink-new.svg/11px-Speakerlink-new.svg.png" width="11" height="11" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Speakerlink-new.svg/17px-Speakerlink-new.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Speakerlink-new.svg/22px-Speakerlink-new.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="11" data-file-height="11" /></a><sup><span style="color: #00e; font: bold 80% sans-serif; padding: 0 .1em;" class="IPA"><a href="/wiki/File:En-us-Guinea.ogg" title="File:En-us-Guinea.ogg">i</a></span></sup></span><span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA_for_English" title="Help:IPA for English">/</a></span><span class="IPA nopopups"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA_for_English#Key" title="Help:IPA for English"><span title="/ˈ/ primary stress follows" style="border-bottom:1px dotted">ˈ</span></a></span><span class="IPA nopopups"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA_for_English#Key" title="Help:IPA for English"><span title="'g' in 'guy'" style="border-bottom:1px dotted">ɡ</span></a></span><span class="IPA nopopups"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA_for_English#Key" title="Help:IPA for English"><span title="/ɪ/ short 'i' in 'bid'" style="border-bottom:1px dotted">ɪ</span></a></span><span class="IPA nopopups"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA_for_English#Key" title="Help:IPA for English"><span title="'n' in 'nigh'" style="border-bottom:1px dotted">n</span></a></span><span class="IPA nopopups"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA_for_English#Key" title="Help:IPA for English"><span title="/i/ 'y' in 'happy'" style="border-bottom:1px dotted">i</span></a></span><span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA_for_English" title="Help:IPA for English">/</a></span></span>, officially the <b>Republic of Guinea</b> (<a href="/wiki/French_language" title="French language">French</a>: <span lang="fr"><i>République de Guinée</i></span>), is a country in <a href="/wiki/West_Africa" title="West Africa">West Africa</a>. Formerly known as <a href="/wiki/French_Guinea" title="French Guinea">French Guinea</a> (<a href="/wiki/French_language" title="French language">French</a>: <span lang="fr"><i>Guinée française</i></span>), it is today sometimes called <b>Guinea-Conakry</b> to distinguish it from its neighbour <a href="/wiki/Guinea-Bissau" title="Guinea-Bissau">Guinea-Bissau</a> and <a href="/wiki/Equatorial_Guinea" title="Equatorial Guinea">Equatorial Guinea</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7"><span>[</span>7<span>]</span></a></sup> Guinea has a population of 10.5 million and an area of 245,860 square kilometres (94,927&#160;sq&#160;mi).<sup id="cite_ref-guinea_stats_1-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-guinea_stats-1"><span>[</span>1<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Guinea is a <a href="/wiki/Republic" title="Republic">republic</a>. The president is directly elected by the people and is <a href="/wiki/Head_of_state" title="Head of state">head of state</a> and <a href="/wiki/Head_of_government" title="Head of government">head of government</a>. The unicameral <a href="/wiki/National_Assembly_(Guinea)" title="National Assembly (Guinea)">Guinean National Assembly</a> is the <a href="/wiki/Legislative_body" title="Legislative body" class="mw-redirect">legislative body</a> of the country, and its members are directly elected by the people. The judicial branch is led by the Guinea Supreme Court, the highest and final court of appeal in the country.<sup id="cite_ref-reuters.com_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-reuters.com-8"><span>[</span>8<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Guinea is a predominantly <a href="/wiki/Islam" title="Islam">Islamic</a> country, with <a href="/wiki/Muslim" title="Muslim">Muslims</a> representing 85 percent of the population.<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9"><span>[</span>9<span>]</span></a></sup> Guinea's people belong to twenty-four <a href="/wiki/Demographics_of_Guinea" title="Demographics of Guinea">ethnic groups</a>. French, the official language of Guinea, is the main language of communication in schools, in government administration, in the media, and among the country's <a href="/wiki/Security_forces" title="Security forces" class="mw-redirect">security forces</a>, but more than twenty-four indigenous languages are also spoken.</p>
<p>Guinea's economy is largely dependent on agriculture and mineral production.<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10"><span>[</span>10<span>]</span></a></sup> It is the world's second largest producer of <a href="/wiki/Bauxite" title="Bauxite">bauxite</a>, and has rich deposits of <a href="/wiki/Diamonds" title="Diamonds" class="mw-redirect">diamonds</a> and <a href="/wiki/Gold" title="Gold">gold</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-guineaconakrysupport.com_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-guineaconakrysupport.com-11"><span>[</span>11<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Human_rights_in_Guinea" title="Human rights in Guinea">Human rights in Guinea</a> remain controversial. In 2011 the United States government claimed that <a href="/wiki/Torture" title="Torture">torture</a> by security forces, and abuse of women and children (e.g. <a href="/wiki/Female_genital_mutilation" title="Female genital mutilation">female genital mutilation</a>) were ongoing abuses of human rights.<sup id="cite_ref-State_2011_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-State_2011-12"><span>[</span>12<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The country is named after the <a href="/wiki/Guinea_(region)" title="Guinea (region)">Guinea region</a>. Guinea is a traditional name for the region of Africa that lies along the <a href="/wiki/Gulf_of_Guinea" title="Gulf of Guinea">Gulf of Guinea</a>. It stretches north through the <a href="/wiki/Tropical_and_subtropical_moist_broadleaf_forests" title="Tropical and subtropical moist broadleaf forests">forested tropical regions</a> and ends at the <a href="/wiki/Sahel" title="Sahel">Sahel</a>. The English term Guinea comes directly from the <a href="/wiki/Portuguese_language" title="Portuguese language">Portuguese</a> word <i>Guiné</i>, which emerged in the mid-15th century to refer to the lands inhabited by the <i>Guineus</i>, a generic term for the black African peoples below the <a href="/wiki/Senegal_River" title="Senegal River">Senegal River</a>, as opposed to the 'tawny' Zenaga Berbers, above it, whom they called <i>Azenegues</i> or <a href="/wiki/Moors" title="Moors">Moors</a>.</p>
<p>The country is currently at the core of the <a href="/wiki/2014_Ebola_outbreak" title="2014 Ebola outbreak" class="mw-redirect">2014 Ebola outbreak</a> giving it a bleak prospect for the upcoming years in terms of humanitarian situation and <a href="/wiki/GDP" title="GDP" class="mw-redirect">GDP</a> growth.</p>
<p></p>

<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Guinea&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_Guinea" title="History of Guinea">History of Guinea</a></div>
<p>The land that is now Guinea belonged to a series of African empires until France colonized it in the 1890s, and made it part of <a href="/wiki/French_West_Africa" title="French West Africa">French West Africa</a>. Guinea declared its independence from France on 2 October 1958. Since independence until the <a href="/wiki/Guinean_presidential_election,_2010" title="Guinean presidential election, 2010">presidential election</a> of 2010, Guinea was governed by a number of <a href="/wiki/Autocracy" title="Autocracy">autocratic</a> rulers.<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13"><span>[</span>13<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14"><span>[</span>14<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15"><span>[</span>15<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="West_African_empires_and_Kingdoms_in_Guinea">West African empires and Kingdoms in Guinea</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Guinea&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: West African empires and Kingdoms in Guinea">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Imamate_of_Futa_Jallon" title="Imamate of Futa Jallon">Imamate of Futa Jallon</a> and <a href="/wiki/Wassoulou_Empire" title="Wassoulou Empire">Wassoulou Empire</a></div>
<p>What is now Guinea was on the fringes of the major <a href="/wiki/West_Africa" title="West Africa">West African</a> empires. The <a href="/wiki/Ghana_Empire" title="Ghana Empire">Ghana Empire</a> is believed to be the earliest of these which grew on trade but contracted and ultimately fell due to the hostile influence of the <a href="/wiki/Almoravids" title="Almoravids" class="mw-redirect">Almoravids</a>. It was in this period that Islam first arrived in the region.</p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Sosso" title="Sosso" class="mw-redirect">Sosso</a> kingdom (12th to 13th centuries) briefly flourished in the void but the Islamic <a href="/wiki/Mandinka_people" title="Mandinka people">Mandinka</a> <a href="/wiki/Mali_Empire" title="Mali Empire">Mali Empire</a> came to prominence when <a href="/wiki/Sundiata_Keita" title="Sundiata Keita">Soundiata Kéïta</a> defeated the Sosso ruler, <a href="/wiki/Soumaoro_Kant%C3%A9" title="Soumaoro Kanté">Soumangourou Kanté</a> at the semi-historical <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Kirina" title="Battle of Kirina">Battle of Kirina</a> in c. 1235. The Mali Empire was ruled by <a href="/wiki/Mansa" title="Mansa">Mansa</a> (Emperors), the most famous being <a href="/wiki/Musa_I_of_Mali" title="Musa I of Mali">Kankou Moussa</a>, who made a famous <a href="/wiki/Hajj" title="Hajj">hajj</a> to Mecca in 1324. Shortly after his reign the Mali Empire began to decline and was ultimately supplanted by its <a href="/wiki/Vassal" title="Vassal">vassal</a> states in the 15th century.</p>
<p>The most successful of these was the <a href="/wiki/Songhai_Empire" title="Songhai Empire">Songhai Empire</a>, expanding its power from about 1460, and eventually surpassing the Mali Empire in both territory and wealth. It continued to prosper until a civil war over succession followed the death of <a href="/wiki/Askia_Daoud" title="Askia Daoud">Askia Daoud</a> in 1582. The weakened empire fell to invaders from <a href="/wiki/Morocco" title="Morocco">Morocco</a> at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Tondibi" title="Battle of Tondibi">Battle of Tondibi</a> just 3 years later. The Moroccans proved unable to rule the kingdom effectively, however, and it split into many small kingdoms.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Almamy_Samory_Tour%C3%A9.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/Almamy_Samory_Tour%C3%A9.jpg/220px-Almamy_Samory_Tour%C3%A9.jpg" width="220" height="291" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/Almamy_Samory_Tour%C3%A9.jpg/330px-Almamy_Samory_Tour%C3%A9.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/7/79/Almamy_Samory_Tour%C3%A9.jpg 2x" data-file-width="346" data-file-height="457" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Almamy_Samory_Tour%C3%A9.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Samori_Ture" title="Samori Ture">Samori Ture</a> was the founder of the <a href="/wiki/Wassoulou_Empire" title="Wassoulou Empire">Wassoulou Empire</a>, an <a href="/wiki/Islamic" title="Islamic" class="mw-redirect">Islamic</a> state in present-day Guinea that resisted <a href="/wiki/France" title="France">French</a> colonial rule in <a href="/wiki/West_Africa" title="West Africa">West Africa</a> from 1882 until Ture's capture in 1898.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>After the fall of the major West African empires, various kingdoms existed in what is now Guinea. <a href="/wiki/Fulani" title="Fulani" class="mw-redirect">Fulani</a> Muslims migrated to <a href="/wiki/Futa_Jallon" title="Futa Jallon" class="mw-redirect">Futa Jallon</a> in Central Guinea and established an Islamic state from 1735 to 1898 with a written constitution and alternate rulers. The Wassoulou or Wassulu empire was a short-lived (1878–1898) empire, led by <a href="/wiki/Samori_Ture" title="Samori Ture">Samori Ture</a> in the predominately <a href="/wiki/Mandinka_people" title="Mandinka people">Malinké</a> area of what is now upper Guinea and southwestern <a href="/wiki/Mali" title="Mali">Mali</a> (Wassoulou). It moved to <a href="/wiki/Ivory_Coast" title="Ivory Coast">Ivory Coast</a> before being conquered by the French.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Colonial_era">Colonial era</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Guinea&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Colonial era">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Slave_trade" title="Slave trade" class="mw-redirect">slave trade</a> came to the coastal region of Guinea with European traders in the 16th century. Slavery had always been part of everyday life but the scale increased as slaves were exported to work elsewhere in the <a href="/wiki/Triangular_trade" title="Triangular trade">triangular trade</a>.</p>
<p>Guinea's colonial period began with French military penetration into the area in the mid-19th century. French domination was assured by the defeat in 1898 of the armies of <a href="/wiki/Samori_Tour%C3%A9" title="Samori Touré" class="mw-redirect">Samori Touré</a>, Mansa (or Emperor) of the <a href="/wiki/Ouassoulou" title="Ouassoulou" class="mw-redirect">Ouassoulou</a> state and leader of Malinké descent, which gave France control of what today is Guinea and adjacent areas.</p>
<p>France negotiated Guinea's present boundaries in the late 19th and early 20th centuries with the British for <a href="/wiki/Sierra_Leone" title="Sierra Leone">Sierra Leone</a>, the Portuguese for their Guinea colony (now <a href="/wiki/Guinea-Bissau" title="Guinea-Bissau">Guinea-Bissau</a>), and <a href="/wiki/Liberia" title="Liberia">Liberia</a>. Under the French, the country formed the <a href="/wiki/French_Guinea" title="French Guinea">Territory of Guinea</a> within <a href="/wiki/French_West_Africa" title="French West Africa">French West Africa</a>, administered by a governor general resident in <a href="/wiki/Dakar" title="Dakar">Dakar</a>. Lieutenant governors administered the individual colonies, including Guinea.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Independence_and_Post-Colonial_Rule_.281958-2008.29">Independence and Post-Colonial Rule (1958-2008)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Guinea&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Independence and Post-Colonial Rule (1958-2008)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In 1958 the <a href="/wiki/French_Fourth_Republic" title="French Fourth Republic">French Fourth Republic</a> collapsed due to political instability and its failures in dealing with its colonies, especially <a href="/wiki/Indochina" title="Indochina" class="mw-redirect">Indochina</a> and <a href="/wiki/Algeria" title="Algeria">Algeria</a>. The founding of a <a href="/wiki/French_Fifth_Republic" title="French Fifth Republic">Fifth Republic</a> was supported by the French people, while French President <a href="/wiki/Charles_de_Gaulle" title="Charles de Gaulle">Charles de Gaulle</a> made it clear on 8 August 1958 that France's colonies were to be given a stark choice between more <a href="/wiki/Autonomous_entity" title="Autonomous entity" class="mw-redirect">autonomy</a> in a new <a href="/wiki/French_Community" title="French Community">French Community</a> and immediate independence in the referendum to be held on 28 September 1958. The other colonies chose the former but Guinea — under the leadership of <a href="/wiki/Ahmed_S%C3%A9kou_Tour%C3%A9" title="Ahmed Sékou Touré">Ahmed Sékou Touré</a> whose <a href="/wiki/Democratic_Party_of_Guinea" title="Democratic Party of Guinea" class="mw-redirect">Democratic Party of Guinea</a> (PDG) had won 56 of 60 seats in 1957 territorial elections — voted overwhelmingly for independence. The French withdrew quickly, and on 2 October 1958, Guinea proclaimed itself a sovereign and independent republic, with Sékou Touré as president.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Ahmed_S%C3%A9kou_Tour%C3%A9_na_obisku_v_Ljubljani_1961_(3).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/Ahmed_S%C3%A9kou_Tour%C3%A9_na_obisku_v_Ljubljani_1961_%283%29.jpg/220px-Ahmed_S%C3%A9kou_Tour%C3%A9_na_obisku_v_Ljubljani_1961_%283%29.jpg" width="220" height="143" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/Ahmed_S%C3%A9kou_Tour%C3%A9_na_obisku_v_Ljubljani_1961_%283%29.jpg/330px-Ahmed_S%C3%A9kou_Tour%C3%A9_na_obisku_v_Ljubljani_1961_%283%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/Ahmed_S%C3%A9kou_Tour%C3%A9_na_obisku_v_Ljubljani_1961_%283%29.jpg/440px-Ahmed_S%C3%A9kou_Tour%C3%A9_na_obisku_v_Ljubljani_1961_%283%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1732" data-file-height="1126" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Ahmed_S%C3%A9kou_Tour%C3%A9_na_obisku_v_Ljubljani_1961_(3).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
President <a href="/wiki/Ahmed_S%C3%A9kou_Tour%C3%A9" title="Ahmed Sékou Touré">Ahmed Sékou Touré</a> was supported by the Communist bloc states and in 1961 visited <a href="/wiki/Yugoslavia" title="Yugoslavia">Yugoslavia</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Following France's withdrawal, Guinea quickly aligned itself with the <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">Soviet Union</a> and adopted <a href="/wiki/Socialism" title="Socialism">socialist</a> policies. This alliance was short lived, however, as Guinea moved towards a Chinese model of socialism. Despite this, however, the country continued to receive aid and investment from <a href="/wiki/Capitalism" title="Capitalism">capitalist</a> countries such as the U.S.. Even the relationship with France improved, after the election of <a href="/wiki/Val%C3%A9ry_Giscard_d%27Estaing" title="Valéry Giscard d'Estaing">Valéry Giscard d'Estaing</a> as president, trade increased and the two countries exchanged diplomatic visits.</p>
<p>By 1960, Touré had declared the PDG the only legal party. For the next 24 years, the government and the PDG were one. Touré was reelected unopposed to four seven-year terms as president, and every five years voters were presented with a single list of PDG candidates for the National Assembly. Advocating a hybrid <a href="/wiki/African_Socialism" title="African Socialism" class="mw-redirect">African Socialism</a> domestically and <a href="/wiki/Pan-Africanism" title="Pan-Africanism">Pan-Africanism</a> abroad, Touré quickly became a polarising leader, and his government became intolerant of dissent, imprisoning hundreds, and stifling free press.</p>
<p>At the same time, the Guinean government nationalised land, removed French appointed and traditional chiefs from power, and broke ties with French government and companies. Vacillating between support for the <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">Soviet Union</a> and (by the late 1970s) the United States, Guinea's economic situation became as unpredictable as its diplomatic line. Alleging plots and conspiracies against him at home and abroad, Touré’s regime targeted real and imagined opponents, driving thousands of political opponents into exile.</p>
<p>In 1970, Portuguese forces, from neighboring <a href="/wiki/Portuguese_Guinea" title="Portuguese Guinea">Portuguese Guinea</a>, staged <a href="/wiki/Operation_Green_Sea" title="Operation Green Sea">Operation Green Sea</a>, a raid into Guinea with the support of exiled Guinean opposition forces. Among other goals, the Portuguese military wanted to kill or capture Sekou Toure due his support of the <a href="/wiki/PAIGC" title="PAIGC" class="mw-redirect">PAIGC</a>, a guerilla movement operating inside Portuguese Guinea.<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16"><span>[</span>16<span>]</span></a></sup> After several days of fierce fighting, the Portuguese forces retreated without achieving most of their goals. The regime of Sékou Touré increased the number of internal arrests and executions.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:22novemberdetail.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/91/22novemberdetail.JPG/220px-22novemberdetail.JPG" width="220" height="362" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/91/22novemberdetail.JPG/330px-22novemberdetail.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/91/22novemberdetail.JPG/440px-22novemberdetail.JPG 2x" data-file-width="455" data-file-height="749" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:22novemberdetail.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Monument to commemorate the 1970 military victory over the Portuguese raid. The only objective not accomplished by the Portuguese raid was the capture of Ahmed Sékou Touré.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Sékou Touré died on 26 March 1984 after a heart operation in the United States, and was replaced by <a href="/wiki/Heads_of_Government_of_Guinea" title="Heads of Government of Guinea" class="mw-redirect">Prime Minister</a> <a href="/wiki/Louis_Lansana_Beavogui" title="Louis Lansana Beavogui">Louis Lansana Beavogui</a>, who was to serve as interim president pending new elections.</p>
<p>The PDG was due to elect a new leader on 3 April 1984. Under the constitution, that person would have been the only candidate for president. However, hours before that meeting, Colonels <a href="/wiki/Lansana_Cont%C3%A9" title="Lansana Conté">Lansana Conté</a> and <a href="/wiki/Diarra_Traor%C3%A9" title="Diarra Traoré">Diarra Traoré</a> seized power in a bloodless <a href="/wiki/Coup" title="Coup" class="mw-redirect">coup</a>. Conté assumed the role of president, with Traoré serving as prime minister until December.</p>
<p>Conté immediately denounced the previous regime’s record on <a href="/wiki/Human_rights" title="Human rights">human rights</a>, released 250 political prisoners and encouraged approximately 200,000 more to return from exile. He also made explicit the turn away from socialism, but this did little to alleviate poverty and the country showed no immediate signs of moving towards <a href="/wiki/Democracy" title="Democracy">democracy</a>.</p>
<p>In 1992, Conté announced a return to civilian rule, with a presidential poll in 1993 followed by elections to parliament in 1995 (in which his party - the <a href="/wiki/Party_of_Unity_and_Progress" title="Party of Unity and Progress" class="mw-redirect">Party of Unity and Progress</a> - won 71 of 114 seats.) Despite his stated commitment to democracy, Conté's grip on power remained tight. In September 2001 the opposition leader <a href="/wiki/Alpha_Cond%C3%A9" title="Alpha Condé">Alpha Condé</a> was imprisoned for endangering state security, though he was pardoned 8 months later. He subsequently spent a period of exile in France.</p>
<p>In 2001 Conté organized and won a referendum to lengthen the presidential term and in 2003 begun his third term after elections were boycotted by the opposition. In January 2005, Conté survived a suspected assassination attempt while making a rare public appearance in the capital <a href="/wiki/Conakry" title="Conakry">Conakry</a>. His opponents claimed that he was a "tired dictator"<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17"><span>[</span>17<span>]</span></a></sup> whose departure was inevitable, whereas his supporters believed that he was winning a battle with dissidents. Guinea still faces very real problems and according to <a href="/wiki/Foreign_Policy" title="Foreign Policy">Foreign Policy</a> is in danger of becoming a <a href="/wiki/Failed_state" title="Failed state">failed state</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18"><span>[</span>18<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>In 2000 Guinea became embroiled in the instability which had long blighted the rest of <a href="/wiki/West_Africa" title="West Africa">West Africa</a> as rebels crossed the borders with <a href="/wiki/Liberia" title="Liberia">Liberia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sierra_Leone" title="Sierra Leone">Sierra Leone</a> and it seemed for a time that the country was headed for <a href="/wiki/Civil_war" title="Civil war">civil war</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19"><span>[</span>19<span>]</span></a></sup> Conté blamed neighbouring leaders for coveting Guinea's natural resources, though these claims were strenuously denied.<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20"><span>[</span>20<span>]</span></a></sup> In 2003 Guinea agreed plans with her neighbours to tackle the insurgents. In 2007 there were <a href="/wiki/2007_Guinean_general_strike" title="2007 Guinean general strike">big protests</a> against the government, resulting in the appointment of a new prime minister.<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21"><span>[</span>21<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Recent_history">Recent history</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Guinea&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Recent history">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Conté remained in power until his death on 23 December 2008<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22"><span>[</span>22<span>]</span></a></sup> and several hours following his death, <a href="/wiki/Moussa_Dadis_Camara" title="Moussa Dadis Camara">Moussa Dadis Camara</a> seized control in a <a href="/wiki/2008_Guinean_coup_d%27%C3%A9tat" title="2008 Guinean coup d'état">coup</a>, declaring himself head of a <a href="/wiki/Military_junta" title="Military junta">military junta</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23"><span>[</span>23<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Protests against the coup became violent and 157 people were killed when on 28 September 2009 the junta ordered its soldiers to attack people who had gathered to protest Camara's attempt to become president.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24"><span>[</span>24<span>]</span></a></sup> The soldiers went on a rampage of rape, mutilation, and murder which caused many foreign governments to withdraw their support for the new regime.<sup id="cite_ref-NYT_25-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-25"><span>[</span>25<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>On 3 December 2009, an aide shot Camara during a dispute about the rampage of September 2009. Camara went to Morocco for medical care.<sup id="cite_ref-NYT_25-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-25"><span>[</span>25<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26"><span>[</span>26<span>]</span></a></sup> Vice-President (and defense minister) <a href="/wiki/S%C3%A9kouba_Konat%C3%A9" title="Sékouba Konaté">Sékouba Konaté</a> flew back from <a href="/wiki/Lebanon" title="Lebanon">Lebanon</a> to run the country in Camara's absence.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27"><span>[</span>27<span>]</span></a></sup> On 12 January 2010 Camara was flown from Morocco to <a href="/wiki/Burkina_Faso" title="Burkina Faso">Burkina Faso</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28"><span>[</span>28<span>]</span></a></sup> After meeting in <a href="/wiki/Ouagadougou" title="Ouagadougou">Ouagadougou</a> on 13 and 14 January, Camara, Konaté and <a href="/wiki/Blaise_Compaor%C3%A9" title="Blaise Compaoré">Blaise Compaoré</a>, President of Burkina Faso, produced a formal statement of twelve principles promising a return of Guinea to civilian rule within six months.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (June 2014)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> It was agreed that the military would not contest the forthcoming elections, and Camara would continue to remain outside of Guinea.<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29"><span>[</span>29<span>]</span></a></sup> On 21 January 2010 the military junta appointed <a href="/wiki/Jean-Marie_Dor%C3%A9" title="Jean-Marie Doré">Jean-Marie Doré</a> as Prime Minister of a six-month transition government, leading up to elections.<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30"><span>[</span>30<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The presidential election was held on 27 June<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31"><span>[</span>31<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-transitional_32-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-transitional-32"><span>[</span>32<span>]</span></a></sup> and was the first free and fair election since independence in 1958. Ex Prime Minister <a href="/wiki/Cellou_Dalein_Diallo" title="Cellou Dalein Diallo">Cellou Dalein Diallo</a> and his rival <a href="/wiki/Alpha_Cond%C3%A9" title="Alpha Condé">Alpha Condé</a> emerged as the two runners-up for the second round.<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33"><span>[</span>33<span>]</span></a></sup> However, due to allegations of electoral fraud, the second round of the election was postponed until 19 September 2010<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34"><span>[</span>34<span>]</span></a></sup> and then again until 10 October.<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35"><span>[</span>35<span>]</span></a></sup> Yet another delay until 24 October was announced in early October.<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36"><span>[</span>36<span>]</span></a></sup> Elections were finally held on 7 November. Voter turnout was high, and the elections went relatively smoothly.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37"><span>[</span>37<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>16 November 2010, <a href="/wiki/Alpha_Cond%C3%A9" title="Alpha Condé">Alpha Condé</a>, the leader of the opposition party <a href="/wiki/Rally_of_the_Guinean_People" title="Rally of the Guinean People">Rally of the Guinean People</a> (RGP), was officially declared the winner and he promised to reform the security sector and review mining contracts.<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38"><span>[</span>38<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>On the night of 18 July 2011, President Condé's residence was attacked in a failed attempted coup.<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39"><span>[</span>39<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40"><span>[</span>40<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>In April 2012, President Condé postponed legislative elections indefinitely, citing the need to ensure that they were "transparent and democratic".<sup id="cite_ref-RNW_Africa_Desk_41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RNW_Africa_Desk-41"><span>[</span>41<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="2013_Protests">2013 Protests</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Guinea&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: 2013 Protests">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The opposition coalition withdrew from the electoral process in mid-February, mainly due to President Conde's insistence on using a suspicious South African firm <a href="/wiki/Waymark_Infotech" title="Waymark Infotech">Waymark Infotech</a> to draw up the registered voter list.<sup id="cite_ref-Reuters_42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Reuters-42"><span>[</span>42<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-SAL_43-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SAL-43"><span>[</span>43<span>]</span></a></sup> In late February 2013, <a href="/wiki/2013_Guinea_Violence" title="2013 Guinea Violence" class="mw-redirect">political violence erupted in Guinea</a> after protesters took to the streets to voice their concerns over the transparency of the upcoming May 2013 elections. The demonstrations were fueled by the opposition coalition’s decision to step down from the electoral process in protest at the lack of transparency in the preparations for elections.<sup id="cite_ref-Rone_44-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rone-44"><span>[</span>44<span>]</span></a></sup> Nine people were killed during the protests, while around 220 were injured, and many of the deaths and injuries were caused by security forces using live fire on protesters.<sup id="cite_ref-Reuters_42-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Reuters-42"><span>[</span>42<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Rtwo_45-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rtwo-45"><span>[</span>45<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The political violence also led to inter-ethnic clashes between the <a href="/wiki/Fula_people" title="Fula people">Fula</a> and <a href="/wiki/Malinke" title="Malinke" class="mw-redirect">Malinke</a> peoples, the latter forming the base of support for President Condé, with the former consisting mainly of the opposition.<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46"><span>[</span>46<span>]</span></a></sup> On 26 March 2013 the opposition party backed out of the negotiation with the government over the upcoming 12 May election. The opposition claimed that the government has not respected them, and have not kept any promises they agreed to. This is expected to lead to more protests and fighting in the streets of Guinea.<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47"><span>[</span>47<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Government_and_Politics">Government and Politics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Guinea&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Government and Politics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Politics_of_Guinea" title="Politics of Guinea">Politics of Guinea</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Alpha_Conde_-_World_Economic_Forum_Annual_Meeting_2012.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Alpha_Conde_-_World_Economic_Forum_Annual_Meeting_2012.jpg/220px-Alpha_Conde_-_World_Economic_Forum_Annual_Meeting_2012.jpg" width="220" height="321" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Alpha_Conde_-_World_Economic_Forum_Annual_Meeting_2012.jpg/330px-Alpha_Conde_-_World_Economic_Forum_Annual_Meeting_2012.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Alpha_Conde_-_World_Economic_Forum_Annual_Meeting_2012.jpg/440px-Alpha_Conde_-_World_Economic_Forum_Annual_Meeting_2012.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3592" data-file-height="5240" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Alpha_Conde_-_World_Economic_Forum_Annual_Meeting_2012.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Alpha_Cond%C3%A9" title="Alpha Condé">Alpha Condé</a>, the current President of Guinea.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The country is a <a href="/wiki/Republic" title="Republic">republic</a>. The president is directly elected by the people and is <a href="/wiki/Head_of_state" title="Head of state">head of state</a> and <a href="/wiki/Head_of_government" title="Head of government">head of government</a>. The Unicameral National Assembly is the <a href="/wiki/Legislative_body" title="Legislative body" class="mw-redirect">legislative body</a> of the country, and its members are directly elected by the people. The judicial branch is led by the Guinea Supreme Court, the highest and final Court of appeal in the country.<sup id="cite_ref-reuters.com_8-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-reuters.com-8"><span>[</span>8<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Guinea is a member of many international organizations including the <a href="/wiki/African_Union" title="African Union">African Union</a>, <a href="/wiki/Agency_for_the_French-Speaking_Community" title="Agency for the French-Speaking Community" class="mw-redirect">Agency for the French-Speaking Community</a>, <a href="/wiki/African_Development_Bank" title="African Development Bank">African Development Bank</a>, <a href="/wiki/Economic_Community_of_West_African_States" title="Economic Community of West African States">Economic Community of West African States</a>, <a href="/wiki/International_Bank_for_Reconstruction_and_Development" title="International Bank for Reconstruction and Development">World Bank</a>, <a href="/wiki/Islamic_Development_Bank" title="Islamic Development Bank">Islamic Development Bank</a>, <a href="/wiki/International_Monetary_Fund" title="International Monetary Fund">IMF</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations" title="United Nations">United Nations</a>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Political_culture">Political culture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Guinea&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Political culture">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>President <a href="/wiki/Alpha_Conde" title="Alpha Conde" class="mw-redirect">Alpha Conde</a> derives support from Guinea's second-largest ethnic group, the <a href="/wiki/Malinke" title="Malinke" class="mw-redirect">Malinke</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Guinea_2013_48-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Guinea_2013-48"><span>[</span>48<span>]</span></a></sup> Guinea's opposition is backed by the <a href="/wiki/Fula_people" title="Fula people">Fula</a> ethnic group, also known as <i>Peul</i>, who account for around 40 percent of the population.<sup id="cite_ref-Guinea_2013_48-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Guinea_2013-48"><span>[</span>48<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Executive_branch">Executive branch</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Guinea&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Executive branch">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/President_of_Guinea" title="President of Guinea" class="mw-redirect">president of Guinea</a> is normally elected by popular vote for a five-year term; candidate must receive a majority of the votes cast to be elected president. The president governs Guinea, assisted by a <a href="/wiki/Privy_Council" title="Privy Council" class="mw-redirect">council</a> of 25 civilian <a href="/wiki/Minister_(government)" title="Minister (government)">ministers</a> appointed by him. The government administers the country through eight regions, 33 <a href="/wiki/Prefecture" title="Prefecture">prefectures</a>, over 100 <a href="/wiki/Subprefecture" title="Subprefecture">subprefectures</a>, and many districts (known as <a href="/wiki/Commune_(subnational_entity)" title="Commune (subnational entity)" class="mw-redirect">communes</a> in <a href="/wiki/Conakry" title="Conakry">Conakry</a> and other large cities and villages or "quartiers" in the interior). District-level leaders are elected; the president appoints officials to all other levels of the highly centralized administration.</p>
<p>Since the 2010 Presidential Elections, the head of state has been <a href="/wiki/Alpha_Cond%C3%A9" title="Alpha Condé">Alpha Condé</a>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Legislative_branch">Legislative branch</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Guinea&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Legislative branch">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/National_Assembly_of_Guinea" title="National Assembly of Guinea" class="mw-redirect">National Assembly of Guinea</a>, the country's legislative body, has not met since 2008 when it was dissolved after the military coup in December. <a href="/wiki/Guinean_legislative_election,_2013" title="Guinean legislative election, 2013">Elections</a> have been postponed many times since 2007. In April 2012, President Condé postponed the elections indefinitely, citing the need to ensure that they were "transparent and democratic".<sup id="cite_ref-RNW_Africa_Desk_41-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RNW_Africa_Desk-41"><span>[</span>41<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Guinean_legislative_election,_2013" title="Guinean legislative election, 2013">2013 Guinean legislative election</a> were held on 24 September 2013.<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49"><span>[</span>49<span>]</span></a></sup> President <a href="/wiki/Alpha_Cond%C3%A9" title="Alpha Condé">Alpha Condé</a>'s party, the <a href="/wiki/Rally_of_the_Guinean_People" title="Rally of the Guinean People">Rally of the Guinean People</a> (RPG), won a <a href="/wiki/Plurality_(voting)" title="Plurality (voting)">plurality</a> of seats in the <a href="/wiki/National_Assembly_of_Guinea" title="National Assembly of Guinea" class="mw-redirect">National Assembly of Guinea</a>, with 53 out of 114 seats. The opposition parties won a total of 53 seats, and opposition leaders denounced the official results as fraudulent.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Foreign_relations">Foreign relations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Guinea&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Foreign relations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Foreign_relations_of_Guinea" title="Foreign relations of Guinea">Foreign relations of Guinea</a></div>
<p>Guinea's foreign relations, including those with its <a href="/wiki/West_Africa" title="West Africa">West African</a> neighbors, have improved steadily since 1985.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50"><span>[</span>50<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Military">Military</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Guinea&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Military">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Military_of_Guinea" title="Military of Guinea">Military of Guinea</a></div>
<p>Guinea's armed forces are divided into five branches – army, navy, air force, the paramilitary National <a href="/wiki/Gendarmerie" title="Gendarmerie">Gendarmerie</a> and the Republican Guard – whose chiefs report to the Chairman of the Joint Chiefs of Staff, who is subordinate to the Minister of Defense. In addition, regime security forces include the National Police Force (Sûreté National). The Gendarmerie, responsible for internal security, has a strength of several thousand.</p>
<p>The army, with about 15,000 personnel, is by far the largest branch of the armed forces. It is mainly responsible for protecting the state borders, the security of administered territories, and defending Guinea's national interests. Air force personnel total about 700. The force's equipment includes several Russian-supplied fighter planes and transports. The navy has about 900 personnel and operates several small <a href="/wiki/Patrol_craft" title="Patrol craft" class="mw-redirect">patrol craft</a> and barges.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Geography">Geography</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Guinea&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Geography">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:652px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Un-guinea.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Un-guinea.png/650px-Un-guinea.png" width="650" height="498" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Un-guinea.png/975px-Un-guinea.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Un-guinea.png/1300px-Un-guinea.png 2x" data-file-width="3092" data-file-height="2367" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Un-guinea.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A map showing <a href="/wiki/List_of_cities_in_Guinea" title="List of cities in Guinea">Guinea's cities</a> and <a href="/wiki/Administrative_divisions_of_Guinea" title="Administrative divisions of Guinea">administrative divisions</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Geography_of_Guinea" title="Geography of Guinea">Geography of Guinea</a></div>
<p>Guinea shares its northern border with Guinea-Bissau, <a href="/wiki/Senegal" title="Senegal">Senegal</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Mali" title="Mali">Mali</a>, and its southern border with <a href="/wiki/Sierra_Leone" title="Sierra Leone">Sierra Leone</a>, <a href="/wiki/Liberia" title="Liberia">Liberia</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Ivory_Coast" title="Ivory Coast">Ivory Coast</a>. The nation forms a crescent as it curves from its western border on the <a href="/wiki/Atlantic_Ocean" title="Atlantic Ocean">Atlantic Ocean</a> toward the east and the south. The sources of the <a href="/wiki/Niger_River" title="Niger River">Niger River</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gambia_River" title="Gambia River">Gambia River</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Senegal_River" title="Senegal River">Senegal River</a> are all found in the <a href="/wiki/Guinea_Highlands" title="Guinea Highlands">Guinea Highlands</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51"><span>[</span>51<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>At 245,857&#160;km<sup>2</sup> (94,926&#160;sq&#160;mi), Guinea is roughly the size of the United Kingdom. There are 320&#160;km (200&#160;mi) of coastline and a total land border of 3,400&#160;km (2,100&#160;mi). Its neighbours are <a href="/wiki/Ivory_Coast" title="Ivory Coast">Ivory Coast</a> (Côte d'Ivoire), <a href="/wiki/Guinea-Bissau" title="Guinea-Bissau">Guinea-Bissau</a>, Liberia, <a href="/wiki/Mali" title="Mali">Mali</a>, Senegal and <a href="/wiki/Sierra_Leone" title="Sierra Leone">Sierra Leone</a>. It lies mostly between latitudes <a href="/wiki/7th_parallel_north" title="7th parallel north">7°</a> and <a href="/wiki/13th_parallel_north" title="13th parallel north">13°N</a>, and longitudes <a href="/wiki/7th_meridian_west" title="7th meridian west">7°</a> and <a href="/wiki/15th_meridian_west" title="15th meridian west">15°W</a> (a small area is west of 15°).</p>
<p>Guinea is divided into four main regions: the <a href="/wiki/Guinean_forest-savanna_mosaic" title="Guinean forest-savanna mosaic">Basse-Coté lowlands</a>, populated mainly by the Susu ethnic group; the cooler, mountainous <a href="/wiki/Fouta_Djallon" title="Fouta Djallon">Fouta Djallon</a> that run roughly north-south through the middle of the country, populated by Fulas, the Sahelian Haute-Guinea to the northeast, populated by <a href="/wiki/Mandinka_people" title="Mandinka people">Malinké</a>, and the forested jungle regions in the southeast, with several ethnic groups. Guinea's mountains are the source for the Niger, the Gambia, and Senegal Rivers, as well as the numerous rivers flowing to the sea on the west side of the range in Sierra Leone and Ivory Coast.</p>
<p>The highest point in Guinea is <a href="/wiki/Mount_Nimba" title="Mount Nimba" class="mw-redirect">Mount Nimba</a> at 1,752&#160;m (5,748&#160;ft). Although the Guinean and Ivorian sides of the <a href="/wiki/Mount_Nimba_Strict_Nature_Reserve" title="Mount Nimba Strict Nature Reserve">Nimba Massif</a> are a <a href="/wiki/UNESCO" title="UNESCO">UNESCO</a> <a href="/wiki/Strict_Nature_Reserve" title="Strict Nature Reserve" class="mw-redirect">Strict Nature Reserve</a>, the portion of the so-called Guinean Backbone continues into <a href="/wiki/Liberia" title="Liberia">Liberia</a>, where it has been mined for decades; the damage is quite evident in the <a href="/wiki/Nz%C3%A9r%C3%A9kor%C3%A9_Region" title="Nzérékoré Region">Nzérékoré Region</a> at <span class="plainlinks nourlexpansion"><a class="external text" href="//tools.wmflabs.org/geohack/geohack.php?pagename=Guinea&amp;params=7_32_17_N_8_29_50_W_region:GN_type:landmark"><span class="geo-default"><span class="geo-dms" title="Maps, aerial photos, and other data for this location"><span class="latitude">7°32′17″N</span> <span class="longitude">8°29′50″W</span></span></span><span class="geo-multi-punct">﻿ / ﻿</span><span class="geo-nondefault"><span class="geo-dec" title="Maps, aerial photos, and other data for this location">7.53806°N 8.49722°W</span><span style="display:none">﻿ / <span class="geo">7.53806; -8.49722</span></span></span></a></span>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Regions_and_prefectures">Regions and prefectures</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Guinea&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Regions and prefectures">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Administrative_divisions_of_Guinea" title="Administrative divisions of Guinea">Administrative divisions of Guinea</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:352px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Guinea_Regions.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Guinea_Regions.png/350px-Guinea_Regions.png" width="350" height="285" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/1/1c/Guinea_Regions.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/1/1c/Guinea_Regions.png 2x" data-file-width="386" data-file-height="314" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Guinea_Regions.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Regions_of_Guinea" title="Regions of Guinea">Regions of Guinea</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The Republic of Guinea covers 245,857 square kilometres (94,926&#160;sq&#160;mi) of West Africa about <a href="/wiki/10th_parallel_north" title="10th parallel north">10 degrees north</a> of the equator. Guinea is divided into four natural regions with distinct human, geographic, and climatic characteristics:</p>
<ul>
<li>Maritime Guinea (<i>La Guinée Maritime</i>) covers 18% of the country</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Middle_Guinea" title="Middle Guinea">Middle Guinea</a> (<i>La Moyenne-Guinée</i>) covers 20% of the country</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Upper_Guinea" title="Upper Guinea">Upper Guinea</a> (<i>La Haute-Guinée</i>) covers 38% of the country</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Guin%C3%A9e_foresti%C3%A8re" title="Guinée forestière">Forested Guinea</a> (<i>Guinée forestière</i>) covers 23% of the country, and is both forested and mountainous</li>
</ul>
<p>Guinea is divided into eight <a href="/wiki/Regions_of_Guinea" title="Regions of Guinea">administrative regions</a> and subdivided into thirty-three <a href="/wiki/Prefectures_of_Guinea" title="Prefectures of Guinea">prefectures</a>. <a href="/wiki/Conakry" title="Conakry">Conakry</a> is Guinea's capital, largest city, and economic centre. <a href="/wiki/Nz%C3%A9r%C3%A9kor%C3%A9" title="Nzérékoré">Nzérékoré</a>, located in the <a href="/wiki/Guin%C3%A9e_foresti%C3%A8re" title="Guinée forestière">Guinée forestière</a> region in Southern Guinea, is the second largest city.</p>
<p>Other major cities in the country with a population above 100,000 include <a href="/wiki/Kankan" title="Kankan">Kankan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kindia" title="Kindia">Kindia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lab%C3%A9" title="Labé">Labe</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gu%C3%A9ck%C3%A9dou" title="Guéckédou">Guéckédou</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bok%C3%A9" title="Boké">Boke</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mamou" title="Mamou">Mamou</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kissidougou" title="Kissidougou">Kissidougou</a>.</p>
